<p><b>William Howard Taft</b> (September 15, 1857 – March 8, 1930) was an American jurist and statesman who served as both the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="List of Presidents of the United States">27th</a> <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President of the United States</a> (1909–1913) and later the 10th <a href="/wiki/Chief_Justice_of_the_United_States" title="Chief Justice of the United States">Chief Justice of the United States</a> (1921–1930). A leader during the <a href="/wiki/Progressive_Era" title="Progressive Era">Progressive Era</a>, Taft and his conservative allies took control of the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_States_Republican_Party" title="History of the United States Republican Party">Republican Party</a> away from Theodore Roosevelt and the liberals. He is the only person to have presided over both the executive and judicial branches of the <a href="/wiki/Federal_government_of_the_United_States" title="Federal government of the United States">United States federal government</a>.</p>
<p>Before becoming President, Taft served as <a href="/wiki/Solicitor_General_of_the_United_States" title="Solicitor General of the United States">Solicitor General of the United States</a> and as a judge on the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Court_of_Appeals_for_the_Sixth_Circuit" title="United States Court of Appeals for the Sixth Circuit">United States Court of Appeals for the Sixth Circuit</a>. In 1900, President <a href="/wiki/William_McKinley" title="William McKinley">William McKinley</a> appointed Taft <a href="/wiki/Governor-General_of_the_Philippines" title="Governor-General of the Philippines">Governor-General of the Philippines</a>. In 1904, President <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore Roosevelt</a> appointed Taft <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_War" title="United States Secretary of War">Secretary of War</a> in an effort to groom Taft, then his close political ally, into his handpicked presidential successor. Taft assumed a prominent role in problem solving, assuming on some occasions the role of acting Secretary of State, while declining repeated offers from Roosevelt to serve on the Supreme Court.</p>
<p>Riding a wave of popular support for fellow <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_States_Republican_Party" title="History of the United States Republican Party">Republican</a> Roosevelt, Taft won an easy victory in his <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1908" title="United States presidential election, 1908">1908 bid for the presidency</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-miller_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-miller-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup> In his only term, Taft's domestic agenda emphasized <a href="/wiki/Trust-busting" title="Trust-busting" class="mw-redirect">trust-busting</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States_civil_service" title="United States civil service" class="mw-redirect">civil service reform</a>, strengthening the <a href="/wiki/Interstate_Commerce_Commission" title="Interstate Commerce Commission">Interstate Commerce Commission</a>, improving the performance of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Postal_Service" title="United States Postal Service">postal service</a>, and passage of the <a href="/wiki/Sixteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Sixteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Sixteenth Amendment</a>. Abroad, Taft sought to further the economic development of nations in Latin America and Asia through "<a href="/wiki/Dollar_Diplomacy" title="Dollar Diplomacy" class="mw-redirect">Dollar Diplomacy</a>", and showed decisiveness and restraint in response to <a href="/wiki/Mexican_Revolution" title="Mexican Revolution">revolution in Mexico</a>. The task-oriented Taft was oblivious to the political ramifications of his decisions, often alienated his own key <a href="/wiki/Interest_group" title="Interest group" class="mw-redirect">constituencies</a>, and was overwhelmingly defeated in his bid for a second term in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1912" title="United States presidential election, 1912">presidential election of 1912</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Historical_rankings_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="Historical rankings of Presidents of the United States">surveys of presidential scholars</a>, Taft is usually ranked near the middle of lists of all American Presidents.</p>
<p>After leaving office, Taft spent his time in academia, arbitration, and the pursuit of world peace through his self-founded <a href="/wiki/League_to_Enforce_Peace" title="League to Enforce Peace">League to Enforce Peace</a>. In 1921, President <a href="/wiki/Warren_G._Harding" title="Warren G. Harding">Warren G. Harding</a> appointed Taft Chief Justice of the United States. He emphasized conservative policies and efficient management.</p>
<p></p>

<ul>
<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-2"><a href="#Legal_career_and_early_politics"><span class="tocnumber">1.1</span> <span class="toctext">Legal career and early politics</span></a></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-3"><a href="#Secretary_of_War_.281904.E2.80.931908.29"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Secretary of War (1904–1908)</span></a></li>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_education">Early life and education</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a href="/wiki/File:William_Howard_Taft_Yale_College_BA_1878.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/William_Howard_Taft_Yale_College_BA_1878.jpg/290px-William_Howard_Taft_Yale_College_BA_1878.jpg" width="290" height="370" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/76/William_Howard_Taft_Yale_College_BA_1878.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/76/William_Howard_Taft_Yale_College_BA_1878.jpg 2x" data-file-width="392" data-file-height="500" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:William_Howard_Taft_Yale_College_BA_1878.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Yale_College" title="Yale College">Yale College</a> photograph of Taft</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>William Howard Taft was born into the powerful <a href="/wiki/Taft_family" title="Taft family">Taft family</a> on September 15, 1857, near Cincinnati, Ohio, the son of <a href="/wiki/Louise_Taft" title="Louise Taft">Louisa Torrey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alphonso_Taft" title="Alphonso Taft">Alphonso Taft</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-LIU_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LIU-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup> His paternal grandfather was <a href="/wiki/Peter_Rawson_Taft" title="Peter Rawson Taft">Peter Rawson Taft</a>, a descendant of <a href="/wiki/Robert_Taft,_Sr" title="Robert Taft, Sr" class="mw-redirect">Robert Taft I</a>, the first Taft in America, who settled in Colonial <a href="/wiki/Mendon,_Massachusetts" title="Mendon, Massachusetts">Mendon</a> in a section known later as <a href="/wiki/Uxbridge,_Massachusetts" title="Uxbridge, Massachusetts">Uxbridge</a>, Massachusetts. Alphonso Taft went to Cincinnati in 1839 to open a law practice,<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup> and was a prominent Republican who served as Secretary of War and <a href="/wiki/United_States_Attorney_General" title="United States Attorney General">Attorney General</a> under President <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant" title="Ulysses S. Grant">Ulysses S. Grant</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Young William attended Cincinnati's <a href="/wiki/First_Unitarian_Church_(Cincinnati,_Ohio)" title="First Unitarian Church (Cincinnati, Ohio)">First Congregational-Unitarian Church</a> with his parents; he joined the congregation at an early age and was an enthusiastic participant. As he rose in the government, he spent little time in Cincinnati. He attended the church much less frequently than he had but worshiped there when he could.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Taft attended <a href="/wiki/Woodward_High_School_(Cincinnati,_Ohio)" title="Woodward High School (Cincinnati, Ohio)">Woodward High School</a> in Cincinnati, and laid the cornerstone of the new Woodward High School, now the site of the <a href="/wiki/School_for_Creative_and_Performing_Arts" title="School for Creative and Performing Arts">School for Creative and Performing Arts</a> (SCPA).<sup id="cite_ref-OHC_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OHC-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> Like others in his family, he attended <a href="/wiki/Yale_College" title="Yale College">Yale College</a> in New Haven, Connecticut.<sup id="cite_ref-EB_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EB-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> At Yale, he was a member of the <a href="/wiki/Linonian_Society" title="Linonian Society">Linonian Society</a>, a literary and debating society; <a href="/wiki/Skull_and_Bones" title="Skull and Bones">Skull and Bones</a>, the secret society co-founded by his father, <a href="/wiki/Alphonso_Taft" title="Alphonso Taft">Alphonso Taft</a>, in 1832; and the Beta chapter of the <a href="/wiki/Psi_Upsilon" title="Psi Upsilon">Psi Upsilon</a> fraternity. He was given the nickname "Big Lub" because of his size,<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> but his college friends knew him by the nickname "Old Bill".<sup id="cite_ref-NYTobit_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTobit-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> While Taft's weight sometimes made him the target of jokes barbed comments, he usually took them with grace and made self-deprecating jokes. Despite his size, he was a good dancer and a better than average athlete who enjoyed golf, tennis and horseback riding.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> Making positive use of his stature, Taft was Yale's intramural heavyweight wrestling champion.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1878, Taft graduated, ranking second in his class out of 121.<sup id="cite_ref-NYTobit_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTobit-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> After college, he attended <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cincinnati_College_of_Law" title="University of Cincinnati College of Law">Cincinnati Law School</a>, graduating with a <a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_Laws" title="Bachelor of Laws">Bachelor of Laws</a> in 1880. While in law school, he worked on the area newspaper <i>The Cincinnati Commercial</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-NYTobit_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTobit-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_career_and_early_politics">Legal career and early politics</span></h3>
<p>After admission to the Ohio <a href="/wiki/Bar_(law)" title="Bar (law)">bar</a>, Taft was appointed Assistant <a href="/wiki/Prosecutor" title="Prosecutor">Prosecutor</a> of <a href="/wiki/Hamilton_County,_Ohio" title="Hamilton County, Ohio">Hamilton County, Ohio</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-NPS_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NPS-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> based in Cincinnati. In 1882, he was appointed local Collector of <a href="/wiki/Internal_Revenue_Service" title="Internal Revenue Service">Internal Revenue</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> Taft married his longtime sweetheart, <a href="/wiki/Helen_Herron_Taft" title="Helen Herron Taft">Helen Herron</a>, in Cincinnati in 1886.<sup id="cite_ref-NPS_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NPS-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1887, he was appointed a judge of the Superior Court of Cincinnati.<sup id="cite_ref-NPS_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NPS-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1890, President <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Harrison" title="Benjamin Harrison">Benjamin Harrison</a> appointed him Solicitor General of the United States;<sup id="cite_ref-NPS_12-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NPS-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> at age 32, he was the youngest-ever Solicitor General.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> Taft then began serving on the newly created United States Court of Appeals for the Sixth Circuit in 1891;<sup id="cite_ref-NPS_12-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NPS-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> he was confirmed by the Senate on March 17, 1892, and received his commission that same day.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> In about 1893, Taft decided in favor of the processing <a href="/wiki/Aluminum" title="Aluminum" class="mw-redirect">aluminum</a> patents belonging to the Pittsburgh Reduction Company, now known as <a href="/wiki/Alcoa" title="Alcoa">Alcoa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> Along with his judgeship, between 1896 and 1900 Taft also served as the first dean and a professor of constitutional law at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cincinnati" title="University of Cincinnati">University of Cincinnati</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1900, President William McKinley appointed Taft chairman of a <a href="/wiki/Taft_Commission" title="Taft Commission">commission to organize a civilian government in the Philippines</a> which had been ceded to the United States by Spain following the <a href="/wiki/Spanish%E2%80%93American_War" title="Spanish–American War">Spanish–American War</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1898)" title="Treaty of Paris (1898)">1898 Treaty of Paris</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NPS_12-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NPS-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> Although Taft had been opposed to the annexation of the islands, and had told McKinley his real ambition was to become a justice of the Supreme Court of the United States, he reluctantly accepted the appointment.<sup id="cite_ref-Miller_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Miller-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>From 1901 to 1904, Taft served as the first civilian <a href="/wiki/Governor-General_of_the_Philippines" title="Governor-General of the Philippines">Governor-General of the Philippines</a>, a position in which he was very popular with both Americans and <a href="/wiki/Filipino_people" title="Filipino people" class="mw-redirect">Filipinos</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Miller_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Miller-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1902, Taft visited Rome to negotiate with <a href="/wiki/Pope_Leo_XIII" title="Pope Leo XIII">Pope Leo XIII</a> for the purchase of Philippine lands owned by the Roman Catholic Church. Taft then persuaded Congress to appropriate more than $7&#160;million to purchase these lands, which he sold to Filipinos on easy terms.<sup id="cite_ref-Miller_18-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Miller-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1903, President Theodore Roosevelt offered Taft the seat on the Supreme Court to which he had for so long aspired, but he reluctantly declined since he viewed the Filipinos as not yet being capable of governing themselves and because of his popularity among them.<sup id="cite_ref-Miller_18-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Miller-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup> This decision was one among many in Taft's career which demonstrated a compulsive dedication to the job at hand, without regard to his self-interest. (Roosevelt actually made the offer of a seat on the Court on several different occasions, being met with a decline every time.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup>) This dedication to the task at hand was the source of much frustration of his political colleagues.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> According to biographer Anderson, contrary to the belief of Roosevelt and other allies, Taft's role as Governor-General in the Philippines did not serve to equip him with the political skills essential for the White House.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Secretary_of_War_.281904.E2.80.931908.29">Secretary of War (1904–1908)</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Taft_Addressing_First_Philippine_Assembly_1907.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Taft_Addressing_First_Philippine_Assembly_1907.jpg/290px-Taft_Addressing_First_Philippine_Assembly_1907.jpg" width="290" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Taft_Addressing_First_Philippine_Assembly_1907.jpg/435px-Taft_Addressing_First_Philippine_Assembly_1907.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Taft_Addressing_First_Philippine_Assembly_1907.jpg/580px-Taft_Addressing_First_Philippine_Assembly_1907.jpg 2x" data-file-width="608" data-file-height="329" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Taft_Addressing_First_Philippine_Assembly_1907.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
William Howard Taft addressing the audience at the <a href="/wiki/Philippine_Assembly" title="Philippine Assembly">Philippine Assembly</a> in the Manila Grand Opera House.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1904, Roosevelt appointed Taft as Secretary of War.<sup id="cite_ref-NPS_12-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NPS-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> This appointment allowed Taft to remain involved in the Philippines and Roosevelt also assured Taft he would support his later appointment to the Court, while Taft agreed to support Roosevelt in the Presidential election of 1904.<sup id="cite_ref-Anderson_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt made the basic policy decisions regarding military affairs, using Taft as a well-traveled spokesman who campaigned for Roosevelt's reelection in 1904. Of Taft's appointment, Roosevelt said, "If only there were three of you; I could appoint one of you to the Court, one to the War Department and one to the Philippines." <sup id="cite_ref-Anderson_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> Taft met with the Emperor of Japan who alerted him of the probability of <a href="/wiki/Russo-Japanese_War" title="Russo-Japanese War">war with Russia</a>. In 1905, Taft met with Japanese Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Katsura_Tar%C5%8D" title="Katsura Tar?">Katsura Tar?</a>. At that meeting, the two signed a secret diplomatic memorandum now called the <a href="/wiki/Taft%E2%80%93Katsura_Agreement" title="Taft–Katsura Agreement" class="mw-redirect">Taft–Katsura Agreement</a>. Contrary to rumor, the memorandum did not establish any new policies but instead repeated the public positions of both nations.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On September 29, 1906, Secretary Taft initiated the <a href="/wiki/Second_Occupation_of_Cuba" title="Second Occupation of Cuba">Second Occupation of Cuba</a> when he established the Provisional Government of Cuba under the terms of the <a href="/wiki/Cuban%E2%80%93American_Treaty_of_Relations_of_1903" title="Cuban–American Treaty of Relations of 1903" class="mw-redirect">Cuban–American Treaty of Relations of 1903</a> (the Platt Amendment), declaring himself Provisional Governor of Cuba.<sup id="cite_ref-archives199_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-archives199-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup> The US sent troops to restore order in <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a> during the revolt led by General Enrique Loynaz del Castillo, and Taft temporarily became the Civil <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_Cuba" title="List of Presidents of Cuba">Governor of Cuba</a>, personally negotiating with Castillo for a peaceful end to the revolt. On October 13, Taft was succeeded as Provisional Governor by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Edward_Magoon" title="Charles Edward Magoon">Charles Edward Magoon</a>. On October 23, President Roosevelt issued <a href="/wiki/Executive_order_(United_States)" title="Executive order (United States)" class="mw-redirect">Executive Order</a> <a href="//en.wikisource.org/wiki/Executive_Order_518" class="extiw" title="s:Executive Order 518">518</a>, ratifying the order and ordering Magoon to report to Taft through the <a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Insular_Affairs" title="Bureau of Insular Affairs">Bureau of Insular Affairs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-archives199_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-archives199-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Also in that year Roosevelt made his third offer to Taft of a position on the Court which he again declined out of a sense of duty to resolve pending issues in the Philippines. Had it been for the Chief Justice seat, a different result may well have ensued.<sup id="cite_ref-Anderson_a_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson_a-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> Taft indicated to Roosevelt he wanted to be Chief Justice of the Supreme Court, not President, but there was no vacancy and Roosevelt had other plans – in 1907 he began touting Taft as the best choice for the Presidential nomination by the party.<sup id="cite_ref-Anderson_a_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson_a-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> Taft's wife was determined to gain the White House and pressured him not to accept a court appointment; other family members also strongly favored the Presidency for him. He gave Taft more responsibilities along with the Philippines and the Panama Canal. For a while, Taft was Acting <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">Secretary of State</a>. When Roosevelt was away, Taft was, in effect, the Acting President.</p>
<p>While serving as the War Secretary Taft generally concentrated on major developments, including the Philippines and the Panama Canal, to the detriment of departmental housekeeping problems, including factionalism within the Department, of which Roosevelt was aware.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1903 the <a href="/wiki/Hay%E2%80%93Bunau-Varilla_Treaty" title="Hay–Bunau-Varilla Treaty">Hay–Bunau-Varilla Treaty</a> granted the U.S. construction rights for the <a href="/wiki/Panama_Canal" title="Panama Canal">Panama Canal</a>, which Roosevelt delegated to the War Department, and Taft thereby supervised the beginning of construction on the Canal.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup> Taft promoted a reduction in the tariffs on sugar and tobacco in the Philippines, a position with which Roosevelt disagreed; Taft offered to resign but this was refused by Roosevelt.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> Taft also had a disagreement with Roosevelt over the latter's conclusion of an executive agreement with the Dominican Republic, in lieu of what Taft thought should have been a treaty, requiring ratification by the Senate. Roosevelt dismissed the complaint as "trifling", and Taft, in his usual style, let it go.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidential_election_of_1908">Presidential election of 1908</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1908" title="United States presidential election, 1908">United States presidential election, 1908</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wm_H_Taft_smiling_1908.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Wm_H_Taft_smiling_1908.jpg/290px-Wm_H_Taft_smiling_1908.jpg" width="290" height="356" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Wm_H_Taft_smiling_1908.jpg/435px-Wm_H_Taft_smiling_1908.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Wm_H_Taft_smiling_1908.jpg/580px-Wm_H_Taft_smiling_1908.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3705" data-file-height="4545" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wm_H_Taft_smiling_1908.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
One of a series of candid photographs known as the <i>Evolution of a Smile,</i> taken just after a formal portrait session, while Taft learns by telephone from Roosevelt that he has been nominated by the Republican Party for the office of President.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1908_Electoral_Map.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/1908_Electoral_Map.png/290px-1908_Electoral_Map.png" width="290" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/1908_Electoral_Map.png/435px-1908_Electoral_Map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/1908_Electoral_Map.png/580px-1908_Electoral_Map.png 2x" data-file-width="1182" data-file-height="635" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1908_Electoral_Map.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Electoral votes by state, 1908.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Taft_inauguration.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Taft_inauguration.jpg/290px-Taft_inauguration.jpg" width="290" height="210" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Taft_inauguration.jpg/435px-Taft_inauguration.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Taft_inauguration.jpg/580px-Taft_inauguration.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="463" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Taft_inauguration.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1909 inauguration</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Theodore Roosevelt had become president after William McKinley was assassinated in 1901. After getting elected president in his own right in 1904, on election night on the lawn of the White House, Roosevelt publicly declared he would not run for <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1908" title="United States presidential election, 1908">reelection in 1908</a>, a decision that he immediately regretted. But he felt bound by his word. Taft was the logical successor, but he was initially reluctant to run, as he had been earlier. As a member of Roosevelt's cabinet, he had declared that his future ambition was to serve on the Supreme Court, not the White House. Taft's efforts in stumping for the party in the 1906 mid-term elections made him aware of his deficiencies as an effective campaigner. Mrs. Taft even commented during this time, "never did he cease to regard a Supreme Court appointment as more desirable than the presidency."<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> But,Taft conceded, with his extensive involvement as the most prominent member of the cabinet, that he was the most "available" man;<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> thus he agreed that were he to be nominated for president, he would put his personal convictions aside and run a vigorous campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>At the time, Roosevelt was convinced that Taft was a genuine "<a href="/wiki/Progressivism" title="Progressivism">progressive</a>" and helped push through the nomination of his Secretary of War onto the Republican ticket on the first ballot at the party convention.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> His opponent in the general election was <a href="/wiki/William_Jennings_Bryan" title="William Jennings Bryan">William Jennings Bryan</a>, who had already run for president twice before, in <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1896" title="United States presidential election, 1896">1896</a> and in <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1900" title="United States presidential election, 1900">1900</a> against <a href="/wiki/William_McKinley" title="William McKinley">William McKinley</a>. During the campaign, Taft undercut Bryan's liberal support by accepting some of his reformist ideas, and Roosevelt's progressive policies blurred the distinctions between the parties. Bryan, on the other hand, ran an aggressive campaign against the nation's business elite. The Democrats referred to Taft's nomination and potential election, pre-determined by the powerful Roosevelt, as a possible "forced succession to the presidency."</p>
<p>It did not take long for Taft's markedly different style, and lack of political acumen, to emerge. <a href="/wiki/Joseph_B._Foraker" title="Joseph B. Foraker">Joseph B. Foraker</a> of Ohio, seeking Taft's support in his senatorial re-election, made an appearance with Taft, creating the impression Taft was allied with the big business trusts. And when Taft failed to follow the Hearst papers in denouncing Foraker's association with them, Roosevelt became incensed.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> Taft also showed his political ineptness by choosing Frank Hitchcock to be Chairman of the Republican Party. Hitchcock was quick to bring in men closely allied with big business, which further alienated the progressive wing of the party.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> Despite the difference in styles, Taft had demonstrated for the most part that the substance of his policies echoed those of Roosevelt.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> In the end, Taft won by a comfortable electoral margin, giving Bryan his worst loss in three presidential campaigns. Taft defeated Bryan by 159 electoral votes; however, he garnered just 51% of the popular vote.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> Mrs. Taft was quoted quite prophetically, saying that, "There was nothing to criticize, except his not knowing or caring about the way the game of politics is played."<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidency.2C_1909_.E2.80.93_1913">Presidency, 1909 – 1913</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width: 220px;">
<div style="width: 220px; height: 270px; overflow: hidden;">
<div style="position: relative; top: -60px; left: -52px; width: 325px"><a href="/wiki/File:TAFT,_William_H-President_(BEP_engraved_portrait).jpg" class="image"><img alt="BEP engraved portrait of Taft as President." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/TAFT%2C_William_H-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/325px-TAFT%2C_William_H-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg" width="325" height="379" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/TAFT%2C_William_H-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/488px-TAFT%2C_William_H-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/TAFT%2C_William_H-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/650px-TAFT%2C_William_H-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="7488" data-file-height="8736" /></a></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:TAFT,_William_H-President_(BEP_engraved_portrait).jpg" title="File:TAFT, William H-President (BEP engraved portrait).jpg">File:TAFT, William H-President (BEP engraved portrait).jpg</a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Engraving_and_Printing" title="Bureau of Engraving and Printing">BEP</a> engraved portrait of Taft as President.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Taft did not enjoy the easy relationship with the press that Roosevelt had, choosing not to offer himself for interviews or photo opportunities as often as the previous president had done.<sup id="cite_ref-american_chronicle_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-american_chronicle-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> When a reporter informed him he was no Teddy Roosevelt, Taft replied that his main goal was to "try to accomplish just as much without any noise".<sup id="cite_ref-american_chronicle_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-american_chronicle-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> Taft even made executive decisions (see below) demonstrating his indifference with the press. Indeed, Taft's administration marked a change in style from the political charisma of Roosevelt to the passion of Taft for the rule of law.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> Taft, in fashioning his cabinet, showed also that he was not unwilling to depart to some degree from Roosevelt's progressivism; he named an anti-progressive, <a href="/wiki/Philander_Chase_Knox" title="Philander Chase Knox" class="mw-redirect">Philander Chase Knox</a> Secretary of State, who had primary influence over other appointments.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Taft considered himself a progressive, in part from his belief in an expansive use of the rule of law, as the prevailing device that should be actively used by judges and others in authority to solve society's, and even the world's, problems. But his devotion to the law also often made Taft a slave to precedent, and less adroit in politics than Roosevelt; he therefore lacked the flexibility, creativity and personal magnetism of his mentor, not to mention the publicity devices, the dedicated supporters, and the broad base of public support that made Roosevelt so formidable.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The divergent views of the two men over the powers of the executive is well articulated in their respective memoirs. In summary, Roosevelt for his part believed 'the President has not just a right but a duty to do anything demanded by the needs of the nation, unless such action is forbidden by the Constitution or federal law." Taft's general opinion on the other hand was that "the President can exercise no power which cannot fairly be traced to some specific grant of power in the Constitution or act of Congress."<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestic_policies_and_politics">Domestic policies and politics</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Anders_L._Zorn_-_William_Howard_Taft_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Anders_L._Zorn_-_William_Howard_Taft_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/260px-Anders_L._Zorn_-_William_Howard_Taft_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="260" height="341" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Anders_L._Zorn_-_William_Howard_Taft_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/390px-Anders_L._Zorn_-_William_Howard_Taft_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Anders_L._Zorn_-_William_Howard_Taft_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/520px-Anders_L._Zorn_-_William_Howard_Taft_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3864" data-file-height="5064" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Anders_L._Zorn_-_William_Howard_Taft_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Official <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a> portrait of William Howard Taft, painted in 1911 by <a href="/wiki/Anders_Zorn" title="Anders Zorn">Anders Zorn</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Keller argues that "Taft in his way was a Progressive president, surpassing TR in antitrust suits and subscribing to an administrative more than political model of the presidency."<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> Taft, however, increasingly came to blows with the Progressive faction of the GOP, which looked to Roosevelt or LaFollette for leadership. Taft thus increasingly depended on the conservative faction of his party.</p>
<p>When President Roosevelt realized that lowering the tariff would divide the Republican Party, he assumed a low profile on that issue. Taft ignored the political dynamite and kept the tariff rates on his agenda (he had raised expectations of lower rates in the campaign); he passively encouraged congressional reformers to draft bills including lower rates, while broadcasting a willingness to compromise with conservative leaders in the Congress, who wanted to keep tariff rates high. Taft described this approach as his "policy of harmony" with the Congress.<sup id="cite_ref-Anderson118122_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson118122-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> The President displayed a more aggressive role early in the drafting of tariff legislation as it regarded the Philippines. He also assumed a similar role in pushing for a corporate income tax. On other matters, he was content to wait until legislation reached its final stage in a joint House–Senate conference committee. Once there, however, he jumped in with both feet, calling each and every member of the committee for a one-on-one meeting at the White House.<sup id="cite_ref-Anderson118122_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson118122-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> The resulting tariff rates in the <a href="/wiki/Payne%E2%80%93Aldrich_Tariff_Act" title="Payne–Aldrich Tariff Act">Payne–Aldrich Tariff Act</a> of 1909 were too high for the progressives, based in part on Taft's campaign promises; but instead of blaming the act's shortcomings on Senator <a href="/wiki/Nelson_W._Aldrich" title="Nelson W. Aldrich">Nelson W. Aldrich</a> and big business, Taft claimed the responsibility, calling it the best bill to come from the Republican Party. Again, due to his results-oriented style, politically he had managed to alienate all sides.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup> The Bureau of Trade Relations later concluded the act overall was moderately successful in lowering rates.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> Congress refused however to fund the Tariff Board which the President included in the Payne–Aldrich Bill, which would have removed the setting of rates from direct continual Congressional manipulation.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Taft was less likely to speak critically of big business than Roosevelt. Nevertheless, his rule-of-law orientation resulted in the filing of 90 <a href="/wiki/Competition_law" title="Competition law">antitrust</a> suits during his administration, compared to 54 such suits by Roosevelt's two-term Justice Department. Taft's efforts included one suit against the country's largest corporation, <a href="/wiki/U.S._Steel" title="U.S. Steel">U.S. Steel</a>, for the acquisition of a Tennessee company during Roosevelt's tenure.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup> The lawsuit even named Roosevelt personally without Taft's knowledge. This was responsible for a complete break with Roosevelt.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> Progressives within the Republican Party began to actively oppose Taft. Senator <a href="/wiki/Robert_M._La_Follette_Sr." title="Robert M. La Follette Sr.">Robert LaFollette</a> of <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin" title="Wisconsin">Wisconsin</a> created the National Progressive Republican League to replace Taft on the national level; although, his campaign crashed after a disastrous speech. Most of LaFollette's supporters went over to Roosevelt. The business community and the conservative wing of the party were also alienated from Taft and contributions to the GOP dried up.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Taft's administration got a political boost after 25 western railroads announced an intent to raise rates by 20%, and Taft responded, first with a threat to enforce the <a href="/wiki/Sherman_Antitrust_Act" title="Sherman Antitrust Act">Sherman Antitrust Act</a> against them; he then negotiated a settlement whereby they agreed to submit delayed rate requests to a new Interstate Commerce Commission having authority over rate requests.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In late 1911, President Taft called for a “central organization in touch with associations and chambers of commerce throughout the country.” Just four months later, on April 22, 1912, Taft created the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Chamber_of_Commerce" title="United States Chamber of Commerce">United States Chamber of Commerce</a> as a counterbalance to the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Labor_history_of_the_United_States" title="Labor history of the United States">labor movement</a> at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Taft's obsession with the law over politics created more trouble for him in the well noted dispute between his Interior Secretary, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Achilles_Ballinger" title="Richard Achilles Ballinger">Richard Achilles Ballinger</a>, and the Chief of the Forestry Service, <a href="/wiki/Gifford_Pinchot" title="Gifford Pinchot">Gifford Pinchot</a>. Ballinger's job was to assure the proper legal form of land withdrawals made from the private sector as part of Roosevelt's conservation policy. Ballinger's review in many instances concluded that the legalities were lacking and lands had to be returned to private owners. Pinchot led the objections to these returns, and even convinced an Interior Department subordinate, <a href="/wiki/Louis_Glavis" title="Louis Glavis">Louis Glavis</a>, to bring an accusation against Ballinger for fraud and collusion with corporate timber interests. Taft refused to intervene until the resulting discord in the cabinet forced him to act. The President reviewed the matter, then fired Glavis and Pinchot; Ballinger also tendered his resignation, which would have further served to end the matter were it not for Taft's refusal to accept it. By that time the political damage had been done, with further alienation of the Progressives from the administration.</p>
<p>Taft, ever reluctant to dismiss cabinet members, nevertheless used the resignations of Ballinger and War Secretary Dickinson to modify the complexion of the cabinet by appointing more progressive Republicans. <a href="/wiki/Walter_L._Fisher" title="Walter L. Fisher">Walter L. Fisher</a>, from the National Conservation League and an ally of Pinchot, replaced Ballinger. <a href="/wiki/Henry_L._Stimson" title="Henry L. Stimson">Henry L. Stimson</a>, another progressive, replaced Dickinson.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> Taft's overriding concern in making most appointments, however, was ability and experience, not party or faction alignment. This was particularly the case with respect to judiciary appointments, specifically in the south, where Taft felt the courts were the weakest.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup> Taft's high standards, which reduced the influence of Senatorial courtesy in the selection process, resulted in the placement of over one hundred well qualified federal judges.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Nevertheless, in the process Taft passed up yet another opportunity to embolden himself politically through the use of patronage.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In the area of federal spending, Taft initiated reforms which would revolutionize the Executive's role in the federal government's budget process. Previously, each executive department presented to the Treasury Dept. its own expense estimates, which were then forwarded to the Congress. Taft ordered each department to begin submitting its requests to the cabinet for review. The first such round of requests and cabinet reviews resulted in a reduction of $92 million, representing the first actual presidential budget in modern history.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup> Taft then requested, and received, approval and funding to create the <a href="/wiki/Commission_on_Economy_and_Efficiency" title="Commission on Economy and Efficiency">Commission on Economy and Efficiency</a> to study the budgeting process. The study recommended the President be required early in the Congressional session to present the legislature with a comprehensive budget. This recommendation ultimately became law with passage of the Budget and Accounting Act of 1921.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Taft's "policy of harmony" with Congress facilitated passage of most of his legislative program. Nevertheless, in the 1910 midterm elections, the Democrats assumed control of the House for the first time in 16 years. At the same time, in the Senate, while the Republicans retained their majority, they lost 8 seats.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Corporate_income_tax">Corporate income tax</span></h4>
<p>To solve an impasse during the 1909 tariff debate, Taft proposed income taxes for corporations and a constitutional amendment to remove the apportionment requirement for taxes on incomes from property (taxes on dividends, interest, and rents), on June 16, 1909.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup> His proposed tax on corporate net income was 1% on net profits over $5,000. It was designated an excise on the privilege of doing business as a corporation whose stockholders enjoyed the privilege of <a href="/wiki/Limited_liability" title="Limited liability">limited liability</a>, and not a tax on incomes as such. In 1911, the Supreme Court, in <i><a href="/wiki/Flint_v._Stone_Tracy_Co." title="Flint v. Stone Tracy Co.">Flint v. Stone Tracy Co.</a></i>, upheld the tax. Receipts grew from $21&#160;million in the fiscal year 1910 to $34.8&#160;million in 1912.</p>
<p>In July 1909, a proposed amendment to allow the federal government to tax incomes was passed unanimously in the Senate and by a vote of 318 to 14 in the House. It was quickly ratified by the states, and on February 3, 1913, it became a part of the Constitution as the <a href="/wiki/Sixteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Sixteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Sixteenth Amendment</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="African_Americans_and_immigrants">African Americans and immigrants</span></h4>
<p>Taft announced in his inaugural address his intention of refusing to appoint African Americans to offices where this would cause race friction. Termed his "Southern Policy", this stance effectively invited white protests wherever blacks were to be, or had already been appointed. Taft made good on his stated intention and removed most black office holders in the South and made only token appointments in the North.<sup id="cite_ref-Tuskegee_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tuskegee-64"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup> Taft was, however, the first president to hire African Americans security guards in the White House.<sup id="cite_ref-Benson_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Benson-65"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Taft met with <a href="/wiki/Booker_T._Washington" title="Booker T. Washington">Booker T. Washington</a> and publicly endorsed his program for the uplifting of black Americans. He advised them to stay out of politics at the time and emphasize education and entrepreneurship. A supporter of free immigration, Taft vetoed a law passed by Congress and supported by labor unions that would have restricted unskilled laborers by imposing a literacy test.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_policy">Foreign policy</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Dollar_Diplomacy" title="Dollar Diplomacy" class="mw-redirect">Dollar Diplomacy</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_American_dreadnought_race#Response:_Argentina_and_Chile.27s_dreadnought_orders" title="South American dreadnought race">Argentine participation in the South American dreadnought race</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:WmHTaft.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/WmHTaft.jpg/260px-WmHTaft.jpg" width="260" height="347" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/WmHTaft.jpg/390px-WmHTaft.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/WmHTaft.jpg/520px-WmHTaft.jpg 2x" data-file-width="695" data-file-height="928" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:WmHTaft.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
President William Howard Taft</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The President surprised the diplomatic arena with his early dismissal of one of the State Department's most experienced diplomats, <a href="/wiki/Henry_White_(diplomat)" title="Henry White (diplomat)">Henry White</a>, the Ambassador to France. The only suspected reason for this decision was that White was thought to have somehow slighted the President and his wife 25 years earlier on their honeymoon in Europe. Taft was oblivious to the serious damage that this decision caused his political reputation.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> (The following year White accepted Taft's appointment to head a delegation to the Pan-American Conference in Buenos Aires.)</p>
<p>The President made it a top priority to reorganize the State Department, saying, "It is organized on the basis of the needs of the government in 1800 instead of 1900."<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup> The Department was for the first time organized into geographical divisions, including the Far East, the Near East, Latin America and Western Europe. This reorganization was engineered in large part by Secretary of State Knox's First Assistant Secretary, Huntington Wilson, who served as de facto Secretary of State due to the frequent absence of Knox. Again displaying his inept administrative leadership, Taft, while not sharing any of Knox's respect for Wilson's ability, deferred to much of Wilson's policy making.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The President personally engaged in talks with the Chinese to provide American assistance in the expansion of the Chinese railroad industry; this was accomplished through participation in the multi-national Hukuang Loan. The effort was dubbed "shirt sleeves diplomacy".<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> Initial success in China led to an extended effort by the President to effect the Open Door Policy, particularly in Manchuria; this was not successful due in large part to the President's reliance on the inexperienced Knox, who failed to properly assess the objections of Japan and Russia.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Taft actively promoted the nation's role in the economic development of Latin America, specifically through the Honduras and Nicaragua conventions. The concept, referred to as "<a href="/wiki/Dollar_Diplomacy" title="Dollar Diplomacy" class="mw-redirect">Dollar Diplomacy</a>", called for the State Department to coordinate loans to the countries for infrastructure improvement from the largest banks in the U.S. Strategically, this was designed to strengthen security for the Panama Canal, increase American trade, and diminish the presence of European nations in the area. Progressives and Insurgent Republicans in the Senate opposed the Wall Street connection, so the effort was largely a failure.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup> The President was more successful in Argentina, where agreements were reached whereby the U.S. provided loans to enable Argentina to acquire battleships; some naval construction and design secrets were sacrificed in the arrangement.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Another of Taft's goals was the furtherance of world peace. He believed that international arbitration between adversarial nations could be utilized as the best means to avoid armed conflict. This was a logical extension of his boundless faith in the rule of law as a Progressive, and it therefore even superseded U.S nationalism as embodied in the Constitution. Hence, he found no objection to surrendering to an international body jurisdiction over the nation's rights in international affairs. As a result, he championed arbitration treaties with Britain and France.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup> The Senate was not prepared to make such a surrender of the nation's interests, and approved the treaties but only with modifications that provided the Senate with a veto over any decisions made in arbitration.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In the 1911 Congressional session Taft's most potentially notable achievement was approval of a reciprocity agreement with Canada which proposed to drastically lower trade barriers. The passage was accomplished with the cooperation of some Democrats, and at a considerable cost of Republican unity.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup> The President confessed to Roosevelt "I think it may break the Republican party for a while." Taft also responded to criticism from party leaders, saying, "I do not give a tinker's dam whether it injures my political prospects or not."<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> Despite the potential benefits of the agreement to the country, which Roosevelt as well understood and anticipated, all was for naught when the Canadian legislature refused to approve it.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Assassination_attempt">Assassination attempt</span></h3>
<p>In 1909, Taft and <a href="/wiki/Porfirio_D%C3%ADaz" title="Porfirio Díaz">Porfirio Díaz</a> planned a summit in El Paso, Texas, and Ciudad Juárez, Mexico, an historic first meeting between a U.S. president and a Mexican president and also the first time an American president would cross the border into Mexico.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup> Diaz requested the meeting to show U.S. support for his planned eighth run as president, and Taft agreed to support Diaz in order to protect the several billion dollars of American capital then invested in Mexico.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> Both sides agreed that the disputed <a href="/wiki/Chamizal_dispute" title="Chamizal dispute">Chamizal strip</a> connecting El Paso to Ciudad Juárez would be considered neutral territory with no flags present during the summit, but the meeting focused attention on this territory and resulted in assassination threats and other serious security concerns.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Texas_Ranger_Division" title="Texas Ranger Division">Texas Rangers</a>, 4,000 U.S. and Mexican troops, U.S. Secret Service agents, <a href="/wiki/Federal_Bureau_of_Investigation" title="Federal Bureau of Investigation">FBI</a> agents and U.S. marshals were all called in to provide security.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup> An additional 250-person private security detail led by <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Russell_Burnham" title="Frederick Russell Burnham">Frederick Russell Burnham</a>, the celebrated scout, was hired by <a href="/wiki/John_Hays_Hammond" title="John Hays Hammond">John Hays Hammond</a>, a close friend of Taft from Yale and a former candidate for U.S. Vice-President in 1908 who, along with his business partner Burnham, held considerable mining interests in Mexico.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup> On October 16, the day of the summit, Burnham and Private C.R. Moore, a Texas Ranger, discovered a man holding a concealed <a href="/wiki/Protector_Palm_Pistol" title="Protector Palm Pistol">palm pistol</a> standing at the El Paso Chamber of Commerce building along the procession route.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup> Burnham and Moore captured and disarmed the would be assassin within only a few feet of Taft and Díaz.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>No foreign affairs controversy tested Taft's statesmanship and commitment to peace more than the subsequent uprising in Mexico against the authoritarian regime of the aging Díaz, which had attracted billions in capital investment for economic development, much of it from the U.S.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup> Anti-regime (and anti-American) riots began in 1910 and were reported by Ambassador <a href="/wiki/Henry_Lane_Wilson" title="Henry Lane Wilson">Henry Lane Wilson</a> to Knox, who failed to pass the information on to the President. Some months later Wilson met with Taft (Knox was out of town on vacation), and upon hearing the information, the President immediately and unilaterally ordered a mobilization of 25,000 troops to the Mexican border as well as naval maneuvers in the Gulf of Mexico. Taft publicly directed that no intervention of troops into Mexico was to occur without Congressional authorization.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup> The President's restraint in the name of peace was difficult to maintain; in Arizona two citizens were killed and almost a dozen injured as a result of the uprising; but Taft would not be goaded into fighting and so instructed the Arizona governor.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1912_presidential_campaign_and_election">1912 presidential campaign and election</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1912" title="United States presidential election, 1912">United States presidential election, 1912</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:For_Auld_Lang_Syne_-_Leonard_Raven-Hill.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/For_Auld_Lang_Syne_-_Leonard_Raven-Hill.jpg/260px-For_Auld_Lang_Syne_-_Leonard_Raven-Hill.jpg" width="260" height="333" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/For_Auld_Lang_Syne_-_Leonard_Raven-Hill.jpg/390px-For_Auld_Lang_Syne_-_Leonard_Raven-Hill.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/For_Auld_Lang_Syne_-_Leonard_Raven-Hill.jpg/520px-For_Auld_Lang_Syne_-_Leonard_Raven-Hill.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1109" data-file-height="1421" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:For_Auld_Lang_Syne_-_Leonard_Raven-Hill.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Taft and Roosevelt – political enemies in 1912</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The results of the 1910 elections made it clear to the President that Roosevelt had departed his camp, and that he might even contend for the party nomination in 1912.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup> On his return from Europe, Roosevelt openly broke with Taft in one of the notable political feuds of the 20th century. To the surprise of observers who thought Roosevelt had unstoppable momentum, Taft determined he would not simply step aside for the popular ex-President, despite the diminished support he had in the party. Taft acknowledged this, saying, "the longer I am President, the less of a party man I seem to become."<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt declared his candidacy for the Republican nomination in February 1912; Taft soon decided that he would focus on canvassing for delegates and not attempt at the outset to take on the more able campaigner one on one.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup> As Roosevelt became more radical in his progressivism, Taft was hardened in his resolve to achieve re-nomination, as he was convinced that the Progressives threatened the very foundation of the government.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup> Taft ultimately outmaneuvered Roosevelt and Senator <a href="/wiki/Robert_M._La_Follette_Sr." title="Robert M. La Follette Sr.">Robert M. La Follette Sr.</a> in delegate count, regained control of the GOP convention; and defeated Roosevelt for the nomination.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-95"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Roosevelt and his group of disgruntled party delegates and members bolted from the party to create the <a href="/wiki/Progressive_Party_(United_States,_1912)" title="Progressive Party (United States, 1912)">Progressive Party</a> (or "Bull Moose") ticket, splitting the Republican vote in the 1912 election.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-95"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup> Taft thought that, despite probable defeat, the party had been preserved as "the defender of conservative government and conservative institutions." He also felt that the expected defeat would remind the party of the need for self-discipline in the face of populist rancor.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson" title="Woodrow Wilson">Woodrow Wilson</a>, the Democrat, was elected with 41% of the popular vote; Roosevelt got 27%, and Taft garnered 23%. Taft won a mere eight electoral votes, in <a href="/wiki/Utah" title="Utah">Utah</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vermont" title="Vermont">Vermont</a>, making it the worst defeat in American history of an incumbent President seeking reelection.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The defeated President had long ago acknowledged his weakness as a campaigner and as well his failure to do the necessary political housekeeping when decisions were made. He also refused to recognize the need to publicize his policies and decisions, saying "After I have made a definite statement, I have to let it go at that until the time for action arises."<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup> Taft's indifference towards the press even extended to legislation, where he failed to recognize the press' need for reduced tariffs on print paper and wood pulp.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup> He further alienated the press when recommending that a deficit in the post office be reduced by eliminating the lower second class rates afforded to magazines and newspapers.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup> Taft commented as follows on the state of his party after the election, "...it behooves the Republicans to gather again to the party standard and pledge anew their faith in their party's principles and to organize again to defend the constitutional government handed down to us by our fathers. Without compromising our principles, we must convince and win back former Republicans, and we must reinforce our ranks with Constitution-loving Democrats." <sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In spite of his failure to be re-elected, Taft achieved what he felt were his main goals as President: keeping permanent control of the party and keeping the courts sacrosanct until they were <a href="/wiki/Judiciary_Reorganization_Bill_of_1937" title="Judiciary Reorganization Bill of 1937" class="mw-redirect">next threatened</a>. While the strife during the election of 1912 devastated the once very close friendship between Taft and Theodore Roosevelt, the two eventually did reconcile not long before Roosevelt's death in 1919.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
