<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Judicial_appointments">Judicial appointments</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Edward_White,_head-and-shoulders_portrait,_facing_slightly_left,_1905.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Edward_White%2C_head-and-shoulders_portrait%2C_facing_slightly_left%2C_1905.jpg/290px-Edward_White%2C_head-and-shoulders_portrait%2C_facing_slightly_left%2C_1905.jpg" width="290" height="369" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Edward_White%2C_head-and-shoulders_portrait%2C_facing_slightly_left%2C_1905.jpg/435px-Edward_White%2C_head-and-shoulders_portrait%2C_facing_slightly_left%2C_1905.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Edward_White%2C_head-and-shoulders_portrait%2C_facing_slightly_left%2C_1905.jpg/580px-Edward_White%2C_head-and-shoulders_portrait%2C_facing_slightly_left%2C_1905.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2585" data-file-height="3288" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Edward_White,_head-and-shoulders_portrait,_facing_slightly_left,_1905.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Taft picked <a href="/wiki/Edward_Douglass_White" title="Edward Douglass White">Edward Douglass White</a> to be <a href="/wiki/Chief_Justice_of_the_United_States" title="Chief Justice of the United States">Chief Justice of the United States</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/William_Howard_Taft_judicial_appointments" title="William Howard Taft judicial appointments" class="mw-redirect">William Howard Taft judicial appointments</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Supreme_Court">Supreme Court</span></h4>
<p>Taft appointed the following justices to the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court of the United States</a>:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Horace_Harmon_Lurton" title="Horace Harmon Lurton">Horace Harmon Lurton</a>&#160;– 1909</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Charles_Evans_Hughes" title="Charles Evans Hughes">Charles Evans Hughes</a>&#160;– 1910</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Edward_Douglass_White" title="Edward Douglass White">Edward Douglass White</a>&#160;– Chief Justice&#160;– 1910</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Willis_Van_Devanter" title="Willis Van Devanter">Willis Van Devanter</a>&#160;– 1911</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Joseph_Rucker_Lamar" title="Joseph Rucker Lamar">Joseph Rucker Lamar</a>&#160;– 1911</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mahlon_Pitney" title="Mahlon Pitney">Mahlon Pitney</a>&#160;– 1912</li>
</ul>
<p>Taft's six appointments to the Court rank below only those of <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a> (who appointed all six justices to the first Court), and of <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a> (who was president for just over twelve years) and equals that of <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a>. Taft's appointment of five new justices tied the number appointed by both <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a> and <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a>. Four of Taft's appointees were relatively young, aged 48, 51, 53, and 54.</p>
<p>The appointments of Edward Douglass White and Charles Evans Hughes also are notable because Taft essentially appointed both his predecessor and successor Chief Justices, respectively. Already on the Court as an associate justice since 1894, White was the first Chief Justice to be elevated from an associate justiceship since President <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a> appointed <a href="/wiki/John_Rutledge" title="John Rutledge">John Rutledge</a> to Chief Justice in 1795. Hughes initially was appointed an <a href="/wiki/Associate_Justice_of_the_Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Associate Justice of the Supreme Court of the United States">Associate Justice</a>, but later resigned to run as the Republican Party's presidential candidate in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1916" title="United States presidential election, 1916">1916 election</a>, which he would lose. President <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover" title="Herbert Hoover">Herbert Hoover</a> renominated Hughes to the Supreme Court as Chief Justice following Taft's retirement.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_judicial_appointments">Other judicial appointments</span></h4>
<p>Besides his Supreme Court appointments, Taft appointed 13 judges to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Courts_of_Appeals" title="United States Courts of Appeals" class="mw-redirect">United States Courts of Appeals</a>, and 38 judges to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_district_courts" title="United States district courts" class="mw-redirect">United States district courts</a>. Taft also appointed judges to various specialty courts, including the first five appointees each to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Commerce_Court" title="United States Commerce Court">United States Commerce Court</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Court_of_Customs_and_Patent_Appeals" title="United States Court of Customs and Patent Appeals">United States Court of Customs Appeals</a>. The Commerce Court was abolished in 1913; Taft was thus the only President to appoint judges to that body.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="States_admitted_to_the_Union">States admitted to the Union</span></h3>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a>: January 6, 1912</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a>: February 14, 1912. Taft insisted that the <a href="/wiki/Recall_election" title="Recall election">recall</a> provision for judges be removed from the state constitution before he would approve it. After it was removed, Taft signed the statehood bill, and state residents promptly put the provision back in.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-presidency">Post-presidency</span></h2>
<p>Upon leaving the White House in 1913, Taft was appointed the Chancellor Kent Professor of Law and Legal History at <a href="/wiki/Yale_Law_School" title="Yale Law School">Yale Law School</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EB_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EB-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> While at Yale, Taft was initiated as an honorary member of the <a href="/wiki/Acacia_Fraternity" title="Acacia Fraternity" class="mw-redirect">Acacia Fraternity</a>. At the same time, Taft was elected president of the <a href="/wiki/American_Bar_Association" title="American Bar Association">American Bar Association</a>. He spent much of his time writing newspaper articles and books, most notably his series on American <a href="/wiki/Jurisprudence" title="Jurisprudence">legal philosophy</a>. He was a vigorous opponent of <a href="/wiki/Prohibition_in_the_United_States" title="Prohibition in the United States">prohibition in the United States</a>, predicting the undesirable situation that the <a href="/wiki/Eighteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Eighteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Eighteenth Amendment</a> would create.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup> He also continued to advocate world peace through international arbitration, urging nations to enter into arbitration treaties with each other and promoting the idea of a <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a> even before the First World War began. Taft was elected a Fellow of the <a href="/wiki/American_Academy_of_Arts_and_Sciences" title="American Academy of Arts and Sciences">American Academy of Arts and Sciences</a> in 1914.<sup id="cite_ref-AAAS_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AAAS-106"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup> Additionally, he lectured on Legal Ethics at <a href="/wiki/Boston_University" title="Boston University">Boston University</a> from 1918 to 1921.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>When World War I did break out in Europe in 1914, however, Taft founded the <a href="/wiki/League_to_Enforce_Peace" title="League to Enforce Peace">League to Enforce Peace</a>. He was a co-chairman of the powerful <a href="/wiki/National_War_Labor_Board" title="National War Labor Board">National War Labor Board</a> between 1917 and 1918.</p>
<p>Taft also supported the war effort by joining the Connecticut <a href="/wiki/United_States_Guards" title="United States Guards">Home Guard</a>, an organization formed to perform many of the in-state duties of the <a href="/wiki/Connecticut_Army_National_Guard" title="Connecticut Army National Guard" class="mw-redirect">National Guard</a> while the National Guard was deployed overseas.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Although he continually advocated peace, he strongly favored <a href="/wiki/Conscription" title="Conscription">conscription</a> once the United States entered the War, pleading publicly that the United States not fight a "finicky" war. He feared the war would be long, but was for fighting it out to a finish, given what he viewed as "Germany's brutality."</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chief_Justice.2C_1921.E2.80.931930">Chief Justice, 1921–1930</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:William_Howard_Taft_as_Chief_Justice_SCOTUS.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/William_Howard_Taft_as_Chief_Justice_SCOTUS.jpg/260px-William_Howard_Taft_as_Chief_Justice_SCOTUS.jpg" width="260" height="346" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/William_Howard_Taft_as_Chief_Justice_SCOTUS.jpg/390px-William_Howard_Taft_as_Chief_Justice_SCOTUS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/William_Howard_Taft_as_Chief_Justice_SCOTUS.jpg/520px-William_Howard_Taft_as_Chief_Justice_SCOTUS.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1148" data-file-height="1526" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:William_Howard_Taft_as_Chief_Justice_SCOTUS.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Chief Justice Taft, ca. 1921</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nomination">Nomination</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:TAFT,_William_H_(signed_check).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/TAFT%2C_William_H_%28signed_check%29.jpg/260px-TAFT%2C_William_H_%28signed_check%29.jpg" width="260" height="114" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/TAFT%2C_William_H_%28signed_check%29.jpg/390px-TAFT%2C_William_H_%28signed_check%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/TAFT%2C_William_H_%28signed_check%29.jpg/520px-TAFT%2C_William_H_%28signed_check%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="1752" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:TAFT,_William_H_(signed_check).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Check signed by Taft on this first day as <a href="/wiki/Chief_Justice_of_the_United_States" title="Chief Justice of the United States">Chief Justice of the United States</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On June 30, 1921, following the death of Chief Justice <a href="/wiki/Edward_Douglass_White" title="Edward Douglass White">Edward Douglass White</a> (whom Taft himself had nominated), President <a href="/wiki/Warren_G._Harding" title="Warren G. Harding">Warren G. Harding</a> nominated Taft to take his place. For a man who had once remarked, "there is nothing I would have loved more than being chief justice of the United States" the nomination to oversee the highest court in the land was like a dream come true.<sup id="cite_ref-Bernard_Schwartz_1993_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bernard_Schwartz_1993-110"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup> There was little opposition to the nomination, and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">Senate</a> approved him 60-4 in a secret session on the day of his nomination, but the <a href="/wiki/Roll_call" title="Roll call">roll call</a> of the vote has never been made public.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup> Taft received his commission immediately and readily took up the position, taking the oath of office on July 11, and serving until 1930. As such, he became the only President to serve as Chief Justice, and thus the only former President to swear in subsequent Presidents, giving the oath of office to both <a href="/wiki/Calvin_Coolidge" title="Calvin Coolidge">Calvin Coolidge</a> (in 1925) and <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover" title="Herbert Hoover">Herbert Hoover</a> (in 1929).</p>
<p>Taft enjoyed his years on the court and was respected by his peers. Justice <a href="/wiki/Felix_Frankfurter" title="Felix Frankfurter">Felix Frankfurter</a> once remarked to Justice <a href="/wiki/Louis_Brandeis" title="Louis Brandeis">Louis Brandeis</a> that it was "difficult for me to understand why a man who is so good a Chief Justice...could have been so bad as President."<sup id="cite_ref-Bernard_Schwartz_1993_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bernard_Schwartz_1993-110"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup> Taft remains the only person to have led both the <a href="/wiki/Executive_(government)" title="Executive (government)">Executive</a> and <a href="/wiki/Judiciary" title="Judiciary">Judicial</a> branches of the <a href="/wiki/Federal_government_of_the_United_States" title="Federal government of the United States">United States government</a>. He considered his time as Chief Justice to be the highest point of his career; allegedly, he once remarked "I do not remember that I was ever President!"<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112"><span>[</span>112<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Taft-Harding-Lincoln.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Taft-Harding-Lincoln.jpg/260px-Taft-Harding-Lincoln.jpg" width="260" height="197" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Taft-Harding-Lincoln.jpg/390px-Taft-Harding-Lincoln.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Taft-Harding-Lincoln.jpg/520px-Taft-Harding-Lincoln.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1428" data-file-height="1082" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Taft-Harding-Lincoln.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Chief_Justice_of_the_United_States" title="Chief Justice of the United States">Chief Justice</a> Taft with President <a href="/wiki/Warren_G._Harding" title="Warren G. Harding">Warren G. Harding</a> and former <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_War" title="United States Secretary of War">Secretary of War</a> <a href="/wiki/Robert_Todd_Lincoln" title="Robert Todd Lincoln">Robert Todd Lincoln</a>, May, 1922</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Accomplishments">Accomplishments</span></h3>
<p>In 1922, Taft traveled to England to study the procedural structure of the English courts and to learn how they dropped such a large number of cases quickly. During the trip, <a href="/wiki/George_V_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George V of the United Kingdom" class="mw-redirect">King George V</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mary_of_Teck" title="Mary of Teck">Queen Mary</a> received Taft and his wife as state visitors.</p>
<p>With what he had learned in England, Taft decided to advocate the introduction and passage of the <a href="/wiki/Judiciary_Act_of_1925" title="Judiciary Act of 1925">Judiciary Act of 1925</a>, which shifts the Supreme Court's appellate jurisdiction to be largely discretionary upon review of litigants' petitioning to be granted an appeal (see also <a href="/wiki/Certiorari" title="Certiorari">writ of certiorari</a>). This allowed the Supreme Court to give preference to what they believed to be cases of national importance and allowed the Court to work more efficiently.</p>
<p>Besides giving the Supreme Court more control over its docket, supporting new legislation, and organizing the Judicial Conference, Taft gave the Supreme Court and the Chief Justice general supervisory power over the scattered and disorganized federal courts.</p>
<p>The legislation also brought the courts of the District of Columbia and of the Territories (and soon, the Commonwealths of the Philippines and <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a>) into the federal court system. This united the courts for the first time as an independent third branch of government under the administrative supervision of the Chief Justice. Taft was also the first Justice to employ two full-time <a href="/wiki/Law_clerk" title="Law clerk">law clerks</a> to assist him.</p>
<p>In 1929, Taft successfully argued in favor of the construction of a separate and spacious <a href="/wiki/United_States_Supreme_Court_Building" title="United States Supreme Court Building">Supreme Court building</a>, reasoning that the Supreme Court needed to distance itself from the Congress as a separate branch of the federal government. Until then, the Court had heard cases in the <a href="/wiki/Old_Senate_Chamber" title="Old Senate Chamber">Old Senate Chamber</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Capitol" title="United States Capitol">Capitol</a>; the Justices had no private chambers there, and their conferences were held in a room in the Capitol's basement. The building was completed in 1935, five years after Taft's death, and remains the seat of the Supreme Court to this day.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Opinions">Opinions</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_Supreme_Court_cases_by_the_Taft_Court" title="List of United States Supreme Court cases by the Taft Court">List of United States Supreme Court cases by the Taft Court</a></div>
<p>While Chief Justice, Taft wrote the opinion for the Court in 256 cases out of the Court's ever-growing caseload. His philosophy of <a href="/wiki/Judicial_interpretation" title="Judicial interpretation">constitutional interpretation</a> was essentially historical <a href="/wiki/Judicial_interpretation#The_contextualist_approach" title="Judicial interpretation">contextualism</a>. Some of his more notable opinions include:</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1925_U.S._Supreme_Court_Justices.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/1925_U.S._Supreme_Court_Justices.jpg/290px-1925_U.S._Supreme_Court_Justices.jpg" width="290" height="198" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/1925_U.S._Supreme_Court_Justices.jpg/435px-1925_U.S._Supreme_Court_Justices.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/90/1925_U.S._Supreme_Court_Justices.jpg 2x" data-file-width="498" data-file-height="340" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1925_U.S._Supreme_Court_Justices.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The U.S. Supreme Court in 1925. Taft is seated in the bottom row, middle.</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Balzac_v._Porto_Rico" title="Balzac v. Porto Rico">Balzac v. Porto Rico</a></i>, 258 <a href="/wiki/United_States_Reports" title="United States Reports">U.S.</a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://supreme.justia.com/cases/federal/us/258/298/">298</a> (1922) (opinion for the Court)
<ul>
<li>Ruling that the <a href="/wiki/Fourteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Fourteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Fourteenth Amendment</a> did not apply the criminal provisions of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Bill_of_Rights" title="United States Bill of Rights">Bill of Rights</a> to overseas territories. This was one of the more famous of the <a href="/wiki/Insular_Cases" title="Insular Cases">Insular Cases</a>.</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Child_Labor_Tax_Case" title="Child Labor Tax Case" class="mw-redirect">Bailey v. Drexel Furniture Co.</a></i>, 259 <a href="/wiki/United_States_Reports" title="United States Reports">U.S.</a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://supreme.justia.com/cases/federal/us/259/20/">20</a> (1922) (opinion for the Court)
<ul>
<li>Holding the <a href="/wiki/The_New_Freedom#Labor" title="The New Freedom">1919 Child Labor Tax Law</a> unconstitutional.</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Hill_v._Wallace" title="Hill v. Wallace">Hill v. Wallace</a></i>, 259 <a href="/wiki/United_States_Reports" title="United States Reports">U.S.</a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://supreme.justia.com/cases/federal/us/259/44/">44</a> (1922) (opinion for the Court)
<ul>
<li>Holding the <a href="/wiki/Future_Trading_Act" title="Future Trading Act">Future Trading Act</a> an unconstitutional use of <a href="/wiki/Taxing_and_Spending_Clause" title="Taxing and Spending Clause">Congress's taxing power</a></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Adkins_v._Children%27s_Hospital" title="Adkins v. Children's Hospital">Adkins v. Children's Hospital</a></i>, 261 <a href="/wiki/United_States_Reports" title="United States Reports">U.S.</a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://supreme.justia.com/cases/federal/us/261/525/">525</a> (1923) (dissenting opinion)
<ul>
<li>Disapproving of the Court's upholding of <i><a href="/wiki/Lochner_v._New_York" title="Lochner v. New York">Lochner v. New York</a></i>. In 1937, the Supreme Court agreed with Taft and reversed this decision permanently.</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Board_of_Trade_of_City_of_Chicago_v._Olsen" title="Board of Trade of City of Chicago v. Olsen">Board of Trade of City of Chicago v. Olsen</a></i>, 262 <a href="/wiki/United_States_Reports" title="United States Reports">U.S.</a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://supreme.justia.com/cases/federal/us/262/1/">1</a> (1923) (opinion for the Court)
<ul>
<li>Upholding the constitutionality of the <a href="/wiki/Grain_Futures_Act" title="Grain Futures Act">Grain Futures Act</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Commerce_Clause" title="Commerce Clause">Commerce Clause</a></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><i>Ex Parte Grossman</i>, 267 <a href="/wiki/United_States_Reports" title="United States Reports">U.S.</a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://supreme.justia.com/cases/federal/us/267/87/">87</a> (1925) (opinion for the Court)
<ul>
<li>Holding that the President's <a href="/wiki/Pardon" title="Pardon">pardon</a> power extends to pardoning people held for criminal contempt. While the Supreme Court rules provide for issuing writs of <a href="/wiki/Habeas_corpus" title="Habeas corpus">habeas corpus</a> within the Court's <a href="/wiki/Original_jurisdiction" title="Original jurisdiction">original jurisdiction</a>, Taft's opinion in <i>Grossman</i> was the last time the Court did so.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Carroll_v._United_States" title="Carroll v. United States">Carroll v. United States</a></i>, 267 <a href="/wiki/United_States_Reports" title="United States Reports">U.S.</a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://supreme.justia.com/cases/federal/us/267/132/">132</a> (1925) (opinion for the Court)
<ul>
<li>Holding that police searches of <a href="/wiki/Automobile" title="Automobile" class="mw-redirect">automobiles</a> without a warrant do not violate the <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Fourth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Fourth Amendment</a> when the police have <a href="/wiki/Probable_cause" title="Probable cause">probable cause</a> to believe that <a href="/wiki/Contraband" title="Contraband">contraband</a> would be found in the automobile</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Samuels_v._McCurdy" title="Samuels v. McCurdy">Samuels v. McCurdy</a></i>, 267 <a href="/wiki/United_States_Reports" title="United States Reports">U.S.</a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://supreme.justia.com/cases/federal/us/267/188/">188</a> (1925) (opinion for the Court)
<ul>
<li>Holding that a prohibition on an item formerly possessed legally is not an <a href="/wiki/Ex_post_facto" title="Ex post facto" class="mw-redirect">ex post facto</a> law, because its possession is an ongoing condition.</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Myers_v._United_States" title="Myers v. United States">Myers v. United States</a></i>, 272 <a href="/wiki/United_States_Reports" title="United States Reports">U.S.</a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://supreme.justia.com/cases/federal/us/272/52/">52</a> (1926) (opinion for the Court)
<ul>
<li>Ruling that the President of the United States had the power to unilaterally dismiss Executive Branch appointees who had been confirmed by the Senate.</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/United_States_v._General_Electric_Co." title="United States v. General Electric Co.">United States v. General Electric Co.</a></i>, 272 <a href="/wiki/United_States_Reports" title="United States Reports">U.S.</a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://supreme.justia.com/cases/federal/us/272/476/">476</a> (1926) (opinion for the Court)
<ul>
<li>Ruling that a <a href="/wiki/United_States_patent_law" title="United States patent law">patentee</a> who has granted a single license to a competitor to manufacture the patented product may lawfully fix the price at which the licensee may sell the product.</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Lum_v._Rice" title="Lum v. Rice">Lum v. Rice</a></i>, 275 <a href="/wiki/United_States_Reports" title="United States Reports">U.S.</a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://supreme.justia.com/cases/federal/us/275/78/">78</a> (1927) (opinion for the Court)
<ul>
<li>Ruling that the <a href="/wiki/Fourteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Fourteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Fourteenth Amendment</a> did not prohibit <a href="/wiki/Mississippi" title="Mississippi">Mississippi</a>'s prevention of Asian children attending <a href="/wiki/White_people" title="White people">white</a> schools during <a href="/wiki/Racial_segregation" title="Racial segregation">racial segregation</a>. The Supreme Court reversed this opinion in 1954.</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Olmstead_v._United_States" title="Olmstead v. United States">Olmstead v. United States</a></i>, 277 <a href="/wiki/United_States_Reports" title="United States Reports">U.S.</a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://supreme.justia.com/cases/federal/us/277/438/">438</a> (1928) (opinion for the Court)
<ul>
<li>Ruling that the judicial practice of excluding evidence obtained without a warrant was based on the <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Fourth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Fourth Amendment's</a> proscription on unreasonable <a href="/wiki/Search_and_seizure" title="Search and seizure">search and seizure</a> but did not apply to <a href="/wiki/Telephone_tapping" title="Telephone tapping">telephone wiretapping</a>.</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Wisconsin_v._Illinois" title="Wisconsin v. Illinois">Wisconsin v. Illinois</a></i>, 278 <a href="/wiki/United_States_Reports" title="United States Reports">U.S.</a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://supreme.justia.com/cases/federal/us/278/367/">367</a> (1929) (opinion for the Court)
<ul>
<li>Holding that the equitable power of the United States can be used to impose positive action on the states in a situation where non-action would result in damage to the interests of other states.</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Old_Colony_Trust_Co._v._Commissioner" title="Old Colony Trust Co. v. Commissioner">Old Colony Trust Co. v. Commissioner</a></i>, 279 <a href="/wiki/United_States_Reports" title="United States Reports">U.S.</a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://supreme.justia.com/cases/federal/us/279/716/">716</a> (1929) (opinion for the Court)
<ul>
<li>Holding that where a third party pays the <a href="/wiki/Income_tax_in_the_United_States" title="Income tax in the United States">income tax</a> owed by an individual, the amount of tax paid constitutes additional income to the taxpayer.</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Medical_conditions_and_weight">Medical conditions and weight</span></h2>
<p>Taft is often remembered as being the most obese president.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup> He was 5&#160;feet 11&#160;inches (1.80&#160;m) tall; his weight peaked at 335–340 pounds (150&#160;kg) toward the end of his Presidency.<sup id="cite_ref-sotos1133_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sotos1133-115"><span>[</span>115<span>]</span></a></sup> The truth of the story of Taft getting stuck in a White House bathtub is unclear. However, he once did overflow a bathtub.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Evidence from <a href="/wiki/Eyewitness_memory" title="Eyewitness memory">eyewitnesses</a>, and from Taft himself, strongly suggests that during his presidency he had severe <a href="/wiki/Sleep_apnea" title="Sleep apnea">obstructive sleep apnea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-sotos1133_115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sotos1133-115"><span>[</span>115<span>]</span></a></sup> His chief symptom was <a href="/wiki/Somnolence" title="Somnolence">somnolence</a>. While President, he fell asleep during conversations, and at the dinner table, and even while standing. He was also strikingly hypertensive, with a systolic blood pressure over 200.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Within a year of leaving the presidency, Taft lost approximately 80 pounds (36&#160;kg). His somnolence problem resolved and, less obviously, his systolic <a href="/wiki/Blood_pressure" title="Blood pressure">blood pressure</a> dropped 40–50 mmHg (from 210 mmHg). Undoubtedly, this weight loss extended his life.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Soon after his weight loss, he had a revival of interest in the outdoors; this led him to explore Alaska.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120"><span>[</span>120<span>]</span></a></sup> Beginning in 1920, Taft used a cane; this was a gift from Professor of Geology W. S. Foster, and was made of 250,000-year-old <a href="/wiki/Petrified_wood" title="Petrified wood">petrified wood</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>After several heart attacks in 1924, Taft was slowing down. He wrote in 1925 that his memory was becoming poorer and in 1928, "my mind does not work as well as it did, and I scatter."<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup> When he administered the <a href="/wiki/Oath_of_Office" title="Oath of Office" class="mw-redirect">Oath of Office</a> to <a href="/wiki/President_Hoover" title="President Hoover" class="mw-redirect">President Hoover</a> on March 4, 1929, he recited part of the oath incorrectly, later writing in a personal letter, "... my memory is not always accurate and one sometimes becomes a little uncertain.", misquoting again in that letter, but differently.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123"><span>[</span>123<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death_and_legacy">Death and legacy</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Taftheadstone.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/63/Taftheadstone.JPG/290px-Taftheadstone.JPG" width="290" height="387" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/63/Taftheadstone.JPG/435px-Taftheadstone.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/6/63/Taftheadstone.JPG 2x" data-file-width="480" data-file-height="640" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Taftheadstone.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Taft's headstone at <a href="/wiki/Arlington_National_Cemetery" title="Arlington National Cemetery">Arlington National Cemetery</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Taft began experiencing hallucinations as 1930 began. On February 3, he stepped down from the Supreme Court. <a href="/wiki/Charles_Evans_Hughes" title="Charles Evans Hughes">Charles Evans Hughes</a>, whom he had appointed as an Associate Justice while President, succeeded him as Chief Justice. An official statement by his doctors announced that he had suffered from heart disease and atherosclerosis for years, but that he had no other serious ailments.</p>
<p>Five weeks following his retirement, some of which was spent in a state of semi-consciousness, Taft died on March 8, 1930, from cardiovascular disease, and the same date as Associate Justice <a href="/wiki/Edward_Terry_Sanford" title="Edward Terry Sanford">Edward Terry Sanford</a>'s unexpected death. As it was customary for members of the court to attend the funeral of deceased members, this posed a "logistical nightmare", necessitating traveling immediately from <a href="/wiki/Knoxville,_Tennessee" title="Knoxville, Tennessee">Knoxville, Tennessee</a>, for Sanford's funeral to Washington for Taft's funeral.<sup id="cite_ref-Christensen_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Christensen-124"><span>[</span>124<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Christensen2_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Christensen2-125"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup> The house at which Taft died is now the diplomatic mission of the Syrian Arab Republic to the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Three days following his death, on March 11, he became the first president and first Chief Justice to be buried at <a href="/wiki/Arlington_National_Cemetery" title="Arlington National Cemetery">Arlington National Cemetery</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Christensen_124-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Christensen-124"><span>[</span>124<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Arlington_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arlington-127"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/James_Earle_Fraser_(sculptor)" title="James Earle Fraser (sculptor)">James Earle Fraser</a> sculpted his grave marker out of <a href="/wiki/Stony_Creek_(Branford)" title="Stony Creek (Branford)">Stony Creek</a> granite.<sup id="cite_ref-Arlington_127-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arlington-127"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup> Taft is one of two presidents buried at Arlington National Cemetery (the other is <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a>), and is one of four Chief Justices buried there (<a href="/wiki/Earl_Warren" title="Earl Warren">Earl Warren</a>, <a href="/wiki/Warren_E._Burger" title="Warren E. Burger">Warren E. Burger</a>, and <a href="/wiki/William_Rehnquist" title="William Rehnquist">William Rehnquist</a> are the others). As a former president, Taft was the only Chief Justice to have had a <a href="/wiki/State_funerals_in_the_United_States" title="State funerals in the United States">state funeral</a>.</p>
<p>In 1938, a third generation of the Taft family entered the national political stage with the election of the former President's oldest son <a href="/wiki/Robert_A._Taft" title="Robert A. Taft">Robert A. Taft I</a> to the Senate, where he became a leader of the conservative Republicans. President Taft's other son, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Phelps_Taft_II" title="Charles Phelps Taft II">Charles Phelps Taft II</a>, served as the mayor of Cincinnati from 1955 to 1957.</p>
<p>President Taft's enduring legacy includes many things named after him. The <a href="/wiki/William_Howard_Taft_National_Historic_Site" title="William Howard Taft National Historic Site">William Howard Taft National Historic Site</a> is the Taft boyhood home. The house in which he was born has been restored to its original appearance. It includes four period rooms reflecting family life during Taft's boyhood, and second-floor exhibits highlighting Taft's life.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128"><span>[</span>128<span>]</span></a></sup> Others include the courthouse of the Ohio Court of Appeals for the First District in Cincinnati; streets in Cincinnati, <a href="/wiki/Arlington,_Virginia" title="Arlington, Virginia" class="mw-redirect">Arlington, Virginia</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Taft_Avenue" title="Taft Avenue">Taft Avenue</a> in <a href="/wiki/Manila" title="Manila">Manila</a>, Philippines; the <a href="/wiki/Taft_Bridge" title="Taft Bridge">Taft Bridge</a> in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a>; a law school in <a href="/wiki/Santa_Ana,_California" title="Santa Ana, California">Santa Ana, California</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129"><span>[</span>129<span>]</span></a></sup> and high schools in <a href="/wiki/San_Antonio" title="San Antonio">San Antonio</a>, Texas; <a href="/wiki/Woodland_Hills,_Los_Angeles,_California" title="Woodland Hills, Los Angeles, California" class="mw-redirect">Woodland Hills</a>, California; Chicago, Illinois; and <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Levin_High_School_for_Media_and_Communications" title="Jonathan Levin High School for Media and Communications">The Bronx</a>. <a href="/wiki/Taft,_Eastern_Samar" title="Taft, Eastern Samar">Taft, Eastern Samar</a>, a town in the Philippines was named after him. After a fire burned much of the town of <a href="/wiki/Taft,_California" title="Taft, California">Moron</a>, California, in the 1920s, it was renamed Taft, California, in his honor.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/George_Burroughs_Torrey" title="George Burroughs Torrey">George Burroughs Torrey</a> painted a portrait of him. Taft is the last President to have sported <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States_with_facial_hair" title="List of Presidents of the United States with facial hair">facial hair while in office</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130"><span>[</span>130<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>

<li id="cite_note-Anderson-23"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Anderson_23-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Anderson_23-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Anderson">Anderson 1973</a>, p. 12.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-24">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See Raymond A. Esthus, "The Taft-Katsura Agreement – Reality or Myth?" <i>Journal of Modern History</i> 1959 31(1): 46–51 <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.jstor.org/stable/1871772">in JSTOR</a>; and Jongsuk Chay, "The Taft-Katsura Memorandum Reconsidered," <i>Pacific Historical Review</i>, Vol. 37, No. 3 (Aug. 1968), pp. 321–326 <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.jstor.org/stable/3636866">in JSTOR</a></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-archives199-25"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-archives199_25-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-archives199_25-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.archives.gov/research/guide-fed-records/groups/199.html">Records of the Provisional Government of Cuba</a>, <a href="/wiki/National_Archives_and_Records_Administration" title="National Archives and Records Administration">National Archives and Records Administration</a>. "Established: By a proclamation of the Secretary of War, September 29, 1906, under general authority of the Permanent Treaty of 1903 between the United States and the Republic of Cuba, with oversight responsibilities assigned to the Bureau of Insular Affairs (War Department) by EO 518, October 23, 1906. ... History: Military Government of Cuba established by Maj. Gen. John R. Brooke, December 28, 1898, as a consequence of U.S. invasion and occupation of Cuba in the Spanish–American War, in accordance with Presidential order published in General Order 184, Headquarters of the Army, December 13, 1898. Spanish colonial administration formally terminated, January 1, 1899. Republic of Cuba established by transfer of sovereignty, May 20, 1902. Domestic unrest in Cuba led to the proclamation of September 29, 1906, which designated Secretary of War William H. Taft as Provisional Governor of Cuba. Taft succeeded as Provisional Governor by Charles E. Magoon, October 13, 1906. EO 518, October 23, 1906, ordered Governor Magoon to report to the Secretary of War through the Bureau of Insular Affairs. Military government terminated January 28, 1909."</span></li>
